&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Recognizing the diversity of religious and secular beliefs held by Texans and by all Americans, it seems beyond peradventure that allowing the seat of government to serve as a stage for the propagation of an unmistakably Judeo-Christian message of piety would have the tendency to make nonmonotheists and nonbelievers "feel like [outsiders] in matters of faith, and [strangers] in the political community."  <em>Pinette</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 799 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., dissenting).  "[D]isplays of this kind inevitably have a greater tendency to emphasize sincere and deeply felt differences among individuals than to achieve an ecumenical goal."  <em>Allegheny County</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S., at 651 (<em>Stevens, J., </em>concurring in part and dissenting in part).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRdissent1.19" href="#FNdissent1.19">19</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even more than the display of a religious symbol on government property, see <em>Pinette</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 797 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., dissenting); <em>Allegheny County</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S., at 650-651 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part), displaying this sectarian text at the state capitol should invoke a powerful presumption of invalidity.  As <em>Justice Souter</em>'s opinion persuasively demonstrates, the physical setting in which the Texas monument is displayed--far from rebutting that presumption--actually enhances the religious content of its message.  See <em>post</em>, at 6-8.  The monument's permanent fixture at the seat of Texas government is of immense significance.  The fact that a monument:</p>
<blockquote><p>"is installed on public property implies official recognition and reinforcement of its message.  That implication is especially strong when the sign stands in front of the seat of government itself.  The 'reasonable observer' of any symbol placed unattended in front of any capitol in the world will normally assume that the sovereign--which is not only the owner of that parcel of real estate but also the lawgiver for the surrounding territory--has sponsored and facilitated its message."  <em>Pinette</em>, 515 U. S., at 801-802 (<em>Stevens</em>, J., dissenting).</p>
</blockquote><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Critical examination of the Decalogue's prominent display at the seat of Texas government, rather than generic citation to the role of religion in American life, unmistakably reveals on which side of the "slippery slope," <em>ante</em>, at 8 (<em>Breyer</em>, J., concurring in judgment), this display must fall.  God, as the author of its message, the Eagles, as the donor of the monument, and the State of Texas, as its proud owner, speak with one voice for a common purpose--to encourage Texans to abide by the divine code of a "Judeo-Christian" God.  If this message is permissible, then the shining principle of neutrality to which we have long adhered is nothing more than mere shadow.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The plurality relies heavily on the fact that our Republic was founded, and has been governed since its nascence, by leaders who spoke then (and speak still) in plainly religious rhetoric.  <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em> cites, for instance, George Washington's 1789 Thanksgiving Proclamation in support of the proposition that the Establishment Clause does not proscribe official recognition of God's role in our Nation's heritage, <em>ante</em>, at 7-8.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRdissent1.20" href="#FNdissent1.20">20</a></sup>  Further, the plurality emphatically endorses the seemingly timeless recognition that our "institutions presuppose a Supreme Being," <em>ante</em>, at 4.  Many of the submissions made to this Court by the parties and <em>amici</em>, in accord with the plurality's opinion,<em> </em>have relied on the ubiquity of references to God throughout our history.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The speeches and rhetoric characteristic of the founding era, however, do not answer the question before us.  I have already explained why Texas' display of the full text of the Ten Commandments, given the content of the actual display and the context in which it is situated, sets this case apart from the countless examples of benign government recognitions of religion.  But there is another crucial difference.  Our leaders, when delivering public addresses, often express their blessings simultaneously in the service of God and their constituents.  Thus, when public officials deliver public speeches, we recognize that their words are not exclusively a transmission from <em>the</em> government because those oratories have embedded within them the inherently personal views of the speaker as an individual member of the polity.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRdissent1.21" href="#FNdissent1.21">21</a></sup>  The permanent placement of a textual religious display on state property is different in kind; it amalgamates otherwise discordant individual views into a collective statement of government approval.  Moreover, the message never ceases to transmit itself to objecting viewers whose only choices are to accept the message or to ignore the offense by averting their gaze.  Cf. <em>Allegheny County</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S., at 664 (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); <em>ante</em>, at 4 (<em>Thomas, J., </em>concurring).  In this sense, although Thanksgiving Day proclamations and inaugural speeches undoubtedly seem official, in most circumstances they will not constitute the sort of governmental endorsement of religion at which the separation of church and state is aimed.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 22" name="FRdissent1.22" href="#FNdissent1.22">22</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The plurality's reliance on early religious statements and proclamations made by the Founders is also problematic because those views were not espoused at the Constitutional Convention in 1787<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRdissent1.23" href="#FNdissent1.23">23</a></sup> nor enshrined in the Constitution's text.  Thus, the presentation of these religious statements as a unified historical narrative is bound to paint a misleading picture.  It does so here.  In according deference to the statements of George Washington and John Adams, <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em> and <em>Justice Scalia,</em> see <em>ante</em>, at 7 (plurality opinion); <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 3-4 (dissenting opinion), fail to account for the acts and publicly espoused views of other influential leaders of that time.  Notably absent from their historical snapshot is the fact that Thomas Jefferson refused to issue the Thanksgiving proclamations that Washington had so readily embraced based on the argument that to do so would violate the Establishment Clause.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRdissent1.24" href="#FNdissent1.24">24</a></sup>  <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em> and <em>Justice Scalia</em> disregard the substantial debates that took place regarding the constitutionality of the early proclamations and acts they cite, see, <em>e.g.,</em> Letter from James Madison to Edward Livingston (July 10, 1822), in 5 The Founders' Constitution 105-106 (P. Kurland &amp; R. Lerner eds. 1987) (hereinafter Founders' Constitution) (arguing that Congress' appointment of Chaplains to be paid from the National Treasury was "not with my approbation" and was a "deviation" from the principle of "immunity of Religion from civil jurisdiction"),<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRdissent1.25" href="#FNdissent1.25">25</a></sup> and paper over the fact that Madison more than once repudiated the views attributed to him by many, stating unequivocally that with respect to government's involvement with religion, the "&nbsp;'tendency to a usurpation on one side, or the other, or to a corrupting coalition or alliance between them, will be best guarded against by an entire abstinence of the Government from interference, in any way whatever, beyond the necessity of preserving public order, &amp; protecting each sect against trespasses on its legal rights by others.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRdissent1.26" href="#FNdissent1.26">26</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;These seemingly nonconforming sentiments should come as no surprise.  Not insignificant numbers of colonists came to this country with memories of religious persecution by monarchs on the other side of the Atlantic.  See A. Stokes &amp; L. Pfeffer, Church and State in the United States 3-23 (rev. ed. 1964).  Others experienced religious intolerance at the hands of colonial Puritans, who regrettably failed to practice the tolerance that some of their contemporaries preached.  <em>Engel</em> v. <em>Vitale</em>, 370 U.&nbsp;S. 421, 427-429 (1962).  <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em> and <em>Justice Scalia</em> ignore the separationist impulses--in accord with the principle of "neutrality"--that these individuals brought to the debates surrounding the adoption of the Establishment Clause.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRdissent1.27" href="#FNdissent1.27">27</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Ardent separationists aside, there is another critical nuance lost in the plurality's portrayal of history.  Simply put, many of the Founders who are often cited as authoritative expositors of the Constitution's original meaning understood the Establishment Clause to stand for a <em>narrower</em> proposition than the plurality, for whatever reason, is willing to accept.  Namely, many of the Framers understood the word "religion" in the Establishment Clause to encompass only the various sects of Christianity.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The evidence is compelling.  Prior to the Philadelphia Convention, the States had begun to protect "religious freedom" in their various constitutions.  Many of those provisions, however, restricted "equal protection" and "free exercise" to Christians, and invocations of the divine were commonly understood to refer to Christ.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRdissent1.28" href="#FNdissent1.28">28</a></sup>  That historical background likely informed the Framers' understanding of the First Amendment.  Accordingly, one influential thinker wrote of the First Amendment that "&nbsp;'[t]he meaning of the term "establishment" in this amendment unquestionably is, the preference and establishment given by law to one sect of Christians over every other.'&nbsp;"  Jasper Adams, The Relation of Christianity to Civil Government in the United States (Feb. 13, 1833) (quoted in Dreisbach 16).  That definition tracked the understanding of the text Justice Story adopted in his famous Commentaries, in which he wrote that the "real object" of the Clause was:</p>
<blockquote><p>"not to countenance, much less to advance Mahometanism, or Judaism, or infidelity, by prostrating Christianity; but to exclude all rivalry among Christian sects, and to prevent any national ecclesiastical establishment, which should give to an hierarchy the exclusive patronage of the national government.  It thus sought to cut off the means of religious persecution, (the vice and pest of former ages,) and the power of subverting the rights of conscience in matters of religion, which had been trampled upon almost from the days of the Apostles to the present age."  2 J. Story, Commentaries on the Constitution of the United States §991, p. 701 (R. Rotunda &amp; J. Nowak eds. 1987) (hereinafter Story); see also <em>Wallace</em>, 472 U.&nbsp;S., at 52-55, and n. 36.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRdissent1.29" href="#FNdissent1.29">29</a></sup></p>
</blockquote><p>
Along these lines, for nearly a century after the Founding, many accepted the idea that America was not just a <em>religious </em>nation, but "a Christian nation."  <em>Church of Holy Trinity</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 143 U.&nbsp;S. 457, 471 (1892).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRdissent1.30" href="#FNdissent1.30">30</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The original understanding of the type of "religion" that qualified for constitutional protection under the Establishment Clause likely did not include those followers of Judaism and Islam who are among the preferred "monotheistic" religions <em>Justice Scalia</em> has embraced in his <em>McCreary County </em>opinion.  See <em>post</em>, at 10-11 (dissenting opinion).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRdissent1.31" href="#FNdissent1.31">31</a></sup>  The inclusion of Jews and Muslims inside the category of constitutionally favored religions surely would have shocked Chief Justice Marshall and Justice Story.  Indeed, <em>Justice Scalia</em> is unable to point to any persuasive historical evidence or entrenched traditions in support of his decision to give specially preferred constitutional status to all monotheistic religions.  Perhaps this is because the history of the Establishment Clause's original meaning just as strongly supports a preference for Christianity as it does a preference for monotheism.  Generic references to "God" hardly constitute evidence that those who spoke the word meant to be inclusive of all monotheistic believers; nor do such references demonstrate that those who heard the word spoken understood it broadly to include all monotheistic faiths.  See <em>supra</em>, at 21.  <em>Justice Scalia</em>'s inclusion of Judaism and Islam is a laudable act of religious tolerance, but it is one that is unmoored from the Constitution's history and text, and moreover one that is patently arbitrary in its inclusion of some, but exclusion of other (<em>e.g.</em>, Buddhism), widely practiced non-Christian religions.  See <em>supra</em>, at 12, 13-14, and n. 16 (noting that followers of Buddhism nearly equal the number of Americans who follow Islam).  Given the original understanding of the men who championed our "Christian nation"--men who had no cause to view anti-Semitism or contempt for atheists as problems worthy of civic concern--one must ask whether <em>Justice Scalia</em> "has not had the courage (or the foolhardiness) to apply [his originalism] principle consistently."  <em>McCreary County, post</em>, at 7.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Indeed, to constrict narrowly the reach of the Establishment Clause to the views of the Founders would lead to more than this unpalatable result; it would also leave us with an unincorporated constitutional provision--in other words, one that limits only the <em>federal </em>establishment of "a national religion."  See <em>Elk Grove Unified School Dist.</em> v. <em>Newdow</em>, 542 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 45 (2004) (<em>Thomas</em>, J., concurring in judgment); cf. A. Amar, The Bill of Rights 36-39 (1998).  Under this view, not only could a State constitutionally adorn all of its public spaces with crucifixes or passages from the New Testament, it would also have full authority to prescribe the teachings of Martin Luther or Joseph Smith as <em>the</em> official state religion.  Only the Federal Government would be prohibited from taking sides, (and only then as between Christian sects).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A reading of the First Amendment dependent on either of the purported original meanings expressed above would eviscerate the heart of the Establishment Clause.  It would replace Jefferson's "wall of separation" with a perverse wall of exclusion--Christians inside, non-Christians out.  It would permit States to construct walls of their own choosing--Baptists inside, Mormons out; Jewish Orthodox inside, Jewish Reform out.  A Clause so understood might be faithful to the expectations of some of our Founders, but it is plainly not worthy of a society whose enviable hallmark over the course of two centuries has been the continuing expansion of religious pluralism and tolerance.  Cf. <em>Abington</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 639, 720, 723 (2002) (<em>Breyer</em>, J., dissenting).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Unless one is willing to renounce over 65 years of Establishment Clause jurisprudence and cross back over the incorporation bridge, see <em>Cantwell</em> v. <em>Connecticut</em>, 310 U.&nbsp;S. 296, 303 (1940), appeals to the religiosity of the Framers ring hollow.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 32" name="FRdissent1.32" href="#FNdissent1.32">32</a></sup>  But even if there were a coherent way to embrace incorporation with one hand while steadfastly abiding by the Founders' purported religious views on the other, the problem of the selective use of history remains.  As the widely divergent views espoused by the leaders of our founding era plainly reveal, the historical record of the preincorporation Establishment Clause is too indeterminate to serve as an interpretive North Star.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRdissent1.33" href="#FNdissent1.33">33</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is our duty, therefore, to interpret the First Amendment's command that "Congress shall make no law respecting an establishment of religion" not by merely asking what those words meant to observers at the time of the founding, but instead by deriving from the Clause's text and history the broad principles that remain valid today.  As we have said in the context of statutory interpretation, legislation "often [goes] beyond the principal evil [at which the statute was aimed] to cover reasonably comparable evils, and it is ultimately the provisions of our laws rather than the principal concerns of our legislators by which we are governed."  <em>Oncale</em> v. <em>Sundowner Offshore Services, Inc.</em>, 523 U.&nbsp;S. 75, 79 (1998).  In similar fashion, we have construed the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to prohibit segregated schools, see <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>, 349 U. S. 294 (1955), even though those who drafted that Amendment evidently thought that separate was not unequal.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 34" name="FRdissent1.34" href="#FNdissent1.34">34</a></sup>  We have held that the same Amendment prohibits discrimination against individuals on account of their gender, <em>Frontiero</em> v. <em>Richardson</em>, 411 U.&nbsp;S. 677 (1973), despite the fact that the contemporaries of the Amendment "doubt[ed] very much whether any action of a State not directed by way of discrimination against the negroes as a class, or on account of their race, will ever be held to come within the purview of this provision," <em>Slaughter-House Cases,</em> 16 Wall. 36, 81 (1873).  And we have construed "evolving standards of decency" to make impermissible practices that were not considered "cruel and unusual" at the founding.  See <em>Roper</em> v. <em>Simmons</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2005) (slip op., at 1) (<em>Stevens</em>, J., concurring).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To reason from the broad principles contained in the Constitution does not, as <em>Justice Scalia</em> suggests, require us to abandon our heritage in favor of unprincipled expressions of personal preference.  The task of applying the broad principles that the Framers wrote into the text of the First Amendment is, in any event, no more a matter of personal preference than is one's selection between two (or more) sides in a heated historical debate.  We serve our constitutional mandate by expounding the meaning of constitutional provisions with one eye towards our Nation's history and the other fixed on its democratic aspirations.  See <em>McCulloch</em> v. <em>Maryland,</em> 4 Wheat. 316, 407, 415 (1819) ("[W]e must never forget, that it is <em>a constitution</em> we are expounding" that is intended to "endure for ages to come, and, consequently, to be adapted to the various <em>crises</em> of human affairs").  Constitutions, after all,</p>
<blockquote><p>"are not ephemeral enactments, designed to meet passing occasions.  They are, to use the words of Chief Justice Marshall, 'designed to approach immortality as nearly as human institutions can approach it.'  The future is their care and provision for events of good and bad tendencies of which no prophecy can be made.  In the application of a constitution, therefore, our contemplation cannot be only of what has been but of what may be.  Under any other rule a constitution would indeed be as easy of application as it would be deficient in efficacy and power.  Its general principles would have little value and be converted by precedent into impotent and lifeless formulas."  <em>Weems</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 217 U. S. 349, 373 (1910).</p>
</blockquote><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The principle that guides my analysis is neutrality.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 35" name="FRdissent1.35" href="#FNdissent1.35">35</a></sup>  The basis for that principle is firmly rooted in our Nation's history and our Constitution's text.  I recognize that the requirement that government must remain neutral between religion and irreligion would have seemed foreign to some of the Framers; so too would a requirement of neutrality between Jews and Christians.  But cf. Letter from George Washington to the Hebrew Congregation in Newport, R.&nbsp;I. (Aug. 18, 1790), in 6 Papers of George Washington 284, 285 (D. Twohig ed. 1996).  Fortunately, we are not bound by the Framers' expectations--we are bound by the legal principles they enshrined in our Constitution.  Story's vision that States should not discriminate between Christian sects has as its foundation the principle that government must remain neutral between valid systems of belief.  As religious pluralism has expanded, so has our acceptance of what constitutes valid belief systems.  The evil of discriminating today against atheists, "polytheists[,] and believers in unconcerned deities," <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 10 (<em>Scalia</em>, J., dissenting), is in my view a direct descendent of the evil of discriminating among Christian sects.  The Establishment Clause thus forbids it and, in turn, forbids Texas from displaying the Ten Commandments monument the plurality so casually affirms.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Eagles may donate as many monuments as they choose to be displayed in front of Protestant churches, benevolent organizations' meeting places, or on the front lawns of private citizens.  The expurgated text of the King James version of the Ten Commandments that they have crafted is unlikely to be accepted by Catholic parishes, Jewish synagogues, or even some Protestant denominations, but the message they seek to convey is surely more compatible with church property than with property that is located on the government side of the metaphorical wall.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The judgment of the Court in this case stands for the proposition that the Constitution permits governmental displays of sacred religious texts.  This makes a mockery of the constitutional ideal that government must remain neutral between religion and irreligion.  If a State may endorse a particular deity's command to "have no other gods before me," it is difficult to conceive of any textual display that would run afoul of the Establishment Clause.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The disconnect between this Court's approval of Texas's monument and the constitutional prohibition against preferring religion to irreligion cannot be reduced to the exercise of plotting two adjacent locations on a slippery slope.  Cf. <em>ante</em>, at 8 (<em>Breyer</em>, J., concurring in judgment).  Rather, it is the difference between the shelter of a fortress and exposure to "the winds that would blow" if the wall were allowed to crumble.  See <em>TVA</em> v. <em>Hill,</em> 437 U.&nbsp;S. 153, 195 (1978) (internal quotation marks omitted).  That wall, however imperfect, remains worth preserving.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I respectfully dissent.</p>

[Graphic omitted: see printed opinion.]

<p></p><hr>

<a name="dissent2"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">THOMAS VAN ORDEN, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> RICK PERRY,<br><em>in his official capacity as </em>GOVERNOR OF TEXAS<br><em>and</em> CHAIRMAN, STATE PRESERVATION<br>BOARD, <em>et&nbsp;al.</em></p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the fifth circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 27, 2005]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Justice O'Connor</em>, dissenting.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For essentially the reasons given by <em>Justice</em> <em>Souter</em>, <em>post,</em> p.___<em> </em>(dissenting opinion), as well as the reasons given in my concurrence in <em>McCreary County</em> v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union of Ky., post, </em>at ___, I respectfully dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<a name="dissent3"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">THOMAS VAN ORDEN, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> RICK PERRY,<br><em>in his official capacity as </em>GOVERNOR OF TEXAS<br><em>and</em> CHAIRMAN, STATE PRESERVATION<br>BOARD, <em>et&nbsp;al.</em></p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the fifth circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 27, 2005]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Justice Souter</em>, with whom <em>Justice Stevens</em> and <em>Justice Ginsburg </em>join, dissenting.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although the First Amendment's Religion Clauses have not been read to mandate absolute governmental neutrality toward religion, cf. <em>Sherbert</em> v. <em>Verner</em>, 374 U.&nbsp;S. 398 (1963), the Establishment Clause requires neutrality as a general rule, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Everson</em> v. <em>Board of Ed. of Ewing</em>, 330 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 18 (1947), and thus expresses Madison's condemnation of "employ[ing] Religion as an engine of Civil policy," Memorial and Remonstrance Against Religious Assessments, 2 Writings of James Madison 183, 187 (G. Hunt ed. 1901).  A governmental display of an obviously religious text cannot be squared with neutrality, except in a setting that plausibly indicates that the statement is not placed in view with a predominant purpose on the part of government either to adopt the religious message or to urge its acceptance by others.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Until today, only one of our cases addressed the constitutionality of posting the Ten Commandments, <em>Stone</em> v. <em>Graham</em>, 449 U.&nbsp;S. 39, 41-42 (1980) <em>(per curiam)</em>.  A Kentucky statute required posting the Commandments on the walls of public school classrooms, and the Court described the State's purpose (relevant under the tripartite test laid out in <em>Lemon</em> v. <em>Kurtzman</em>, 403 U.&nbsp;S. 602 (1971)) as being at odds with the obligation of religious neutrality.</p>
<blockquote><p>"The pre-eminent purpose for posting the Ten Commandments on schoolroom walls is plainly religious in nature.  The Ten Commandments are undeniably a sacred text in the Jewish and Christian faiths, and no legislative recitation of a supposed secular purpose can blind us to that fact.  The Commandments do not confine themselves to arguably secular matters, such as honoring one's parents, killing or murder, adultery, stealing, false witness, and covetousness. Rather, the first part of the Commandments concerns the religious duties of believers: worshipping the Lord God alone, avoiding idolatry, not using the Lord's name in vain, and observing the Sabbath Day."  449 U.&nbsp;S, at 41-42 (footnote and citations omitted).</p>
</blockquote><p>
What these observations underscore are the simple realities that the Ten Commandments constitute a religious statement, that their message is inherently religious, and that the purpose of singling them out in a display is clearly the same.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRdissent3.1" href="#FNdissent3.1">1</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, a pedestrian happening upon the monument at issue here needs no training in religious doctrine to realize that the statement of the Commandments, quoting God himself, proclaims that the will of the divine being is the source of obligation to obey the rules, including the facially secular ones.  In this case, moreover, the text is presented to give particular prominence to the Commandments' first sectarian reference, "I am the Lord thy God."  That proclamation is centered on the stone and written in slightly larger letters than the subsequent recitation.  To ensure that the religious nature of the monument is clear to even the most casual passerby, the word "Lord" appears in all capital letters (as does the word "am"), so that the most eye-catching segment of the quotation is the declaration "I AM the LORD thy God."  App. to Pet. for Cert. 21.  What follows, of course, are the rules against other gods, graven images, vain swearing, and Sabbath breaking.  And the full text of the fifth Commandment puts forward filial respect as a condition of long life in the land "which the Lord they God giveth thee."  See <em>ibid.</em>  These "[w]ords ... make [the] ... religious meaning unmistakably clear."  <em>County of Allegheny </em>v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union, Greater Pittsburgh Chapter,</em> 492 U.&nbsp;S. 573, 598 (1989).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To drive the religious point home, and identify the message as religious to any viewer who failed to read the text, the engraved quotation is framed by religious symbols: two tablets with what appears to be ancient script on them, two Stars of David, and the superimposed Greek letters Chi and Rho as the familiar monogram of Christ.  Nothing on the monument, in fact, detracts from its religious nature,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRdissent3.2" href="#FNdissent3.2">2</a></sup> see <em>ibid.</em> ("Here, unlike in <em>Lynch</em> [v. <em>Donnelly</em>, 465 U. S. 668 (1984)], nothing in the context of the display detracts from the cr&amp;ccaron;che's religious message"), and the plurality does not suggest otherwise.  It would therefore be difficult to miss the point that the government of Texas<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRdissent3.3" href="#FNdissent3.3">3</a></sup> is telling everyone who sees the monument to live up to a moral code because God requires it, with both code and conception of God being rightly understood as the inheritances specifically of Jews and Christians.  And it is likewise unsurprising that the District Court expressly rejected Texas's argument that the State's purpose in placing the monument on the capitol grounds was related to the Commandments' role as "part of the foundation of modern secular law in Texas and elsewhere."  App. to Pet. for Cert. 32.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The monument's presentation of the Commandments with<strong> </strong>religious text emphasized and enhanced stands in contrast to any number of perfectly constitutional depictions of them, the frieze of our own Courtroom providing a good example, where the figure of Moses stands among history's great lawgivers.  While Moses holds the tablets of the Commandments showing some Hebrew text, no one looking at the lines of figures in marble relief is likely to see a religious purpose behind the assemblage or take away a religious message from it.  Only one other depiction represents a religious leader, and the historical personages are mixed with symbols of moral and intellectual abstractions like Equity and Authority.  See <em>County of Allegheny</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 652 (<em>Stevens, J.</em>, concurring in part and dissenting in part).  Since Moses enjoys no especial prominence on the frieze, viewers can readily take him to be there as a lawgiver in the company of other lawgivers; and the viewers may just as naturally see the tablets of the Commandments (showing the later ones, forbidding things like killing and theft, but without the divine preface) as background from which the concept of law emerged, ultimately having a secular influence in the history of the Nation.  Government may, of course, constitutionally call attention to this influence, and may post displays or erect monuments recounting this aspect of our history no less than any<sup> </sup>other, so long as there is a context and that context is historical.  Hence, a display of the Commandments accompanied by an exposition of how they have influenced modern law would most likely be constitutionally unobjectionable.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 4" name="FRdissent3.4" href="#FNdissent3.4">4</a></sup>  And the Decalogue could, as <em>Stone</em> suggested, be integrated constitutionally into a course of study in public schools.  <em>Stone, </em>449 U.&nbsp;S.<em>,</em> at 42.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 5" name="FRdissent3.5" href="#FNdissent3.5">5</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Texas seeks to take advantage of the recognition that visual symbol and written text can manifest a secular purpose in secular company, when it argues that its monument (like Moses in the frieze) is not alone and ought to be viewed as only 1 among 17 placed on the 22 acres surrounding the state capitol.  Texas, indeed, says that the Capitol grounds are like a museum for a collection of exhibits, the kind of setting that several Members of the Court have said can render the exhibition of religious artifacts permissible, even though in other circumstances their display would be seen as meant to convey a religious message forbidden to the State.  <em>County of Allegheny, </em>465 U. S. 668, 692 (1984) (<em>O'Connor,</em> J., concurring).  So, for example, the Government of the United States does not violate the Establishment Clause by hanging Giotto's Madonna on the wall of the National Gallery.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But 17 monuments with no common appearance, history, or esthetic role scattered over 22 acres is not a museum, and anyone strolling around the lawn would surely take each memorial on its own terms without any dawning sense that some purpose held the miscellany together more coherently than fortuity and the edge of the grass.  One monument expresses admiration for pioneer women.  One pays respect to the fighters of World War II.  And one quotes the God of Abraham whose command is the sanction for moral law.  The themes are individual grit, patriotic courage, and God as the source of Jewish and Christian morality; there is no common denominator.  In like circumstances, we rejected an argument similar to the State's, noting in <em>County of Allegheny</em> that "[t]he presence of Santas or other Christmas decorations elsewhere in the . . . [c]ourthouse, and of the nearby gallery forum, fail to negate the [cr&amp;ccaron;che's] endorsement effect. . . .  The record demonstrates . . . that the cr&amp;ccaron;che, with its floral frame, was its own display distinct from any other decorations or exhibitions in the building."  492 U.&nbsp;S., at 598-599, n. 48.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRdissent3.6" href="#FNdissent3.6">6</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If the State's museum argument does nothing to blunt the religious message and manifestly religious purpose behind it, neither does the plurality's reliance on generalities culled from cases factually different from this one.  <em>E.g., ante,</em> at 8 ("We have acknowledged, for example, that 'religion has been closely identified with our history and government,' <em>School Dist. of Abington Township</em> v. <em>Schempp,</em> 370 U.&nbsp;S. 421, 434 (1962)").  In fact, it is not until the end of its opinion that the plurality turns to the relevant precedent of <em>Stone</em>, a case actually dealing with a display of the Decalogue.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When the plurality finally does confront <em>Stone,</em> it tries to avoid the case's obvious applicability by limiting its holding to the classroom setting.  The plurality claims to find authority for limiting <em>Stone</em>'s reach this way in the opinion's citations of two school-prayer cases, <em>School Dist. of Abington Township</em> v. <em>Schempp,</em> 374 U.&nbsp;S. 203 (1963), and <em>Engel</em> v. <em>Vitale,</em> 370 U.&nbsp;S. 421 (1962).  But <em>Stone </em>relied on those cases for widely applicable notions, not for any concept specific to schools.  The opinion quoted <em>Schempp</em>'s<em> </em>statements that "it is no defense to urge that the religious practices here may be relatively minor encroachments on the First Amendment," <em>Schempp, supra,</em> at 225, quoted in <em>Stone, </em>482 U.&nbsp;S. 578, 584 (1987).  <em>Stone</em> did not, for example, speak of children's impressionability or their captivity as an audience in a school class.  In fact, <em>Stone</em>'s reasoning reached the classroom only in noting the lack of support for the claim that the State had brought the Commandments into schools in order to "integrat[e] [them] into the school curriculum."  449 U.&nbsp;S.<em>,</em> at 42.  Accordingly, our numerous prior discussions of <em>Stone</em> have never treated its holding as restricted to the classroom.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 7" name="FRdissent3.7" href="#FNdissent3.7">7</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor can the plurality deflect <em>Stone</em> by calling the Texas monument "a far more passive use of [the Decalogue] than was the case in <em>Stone,</em> where the text confronted elementary school students every day."  <em>Ante,</em> at 12.  Placing a monument on the ground is not more "passive" than hanging a sheet of paper on a wall when both contain the same text to be read by anyone who<strong> </strong>looks at it.  The problem in <em>Stone</em> was simply that the State was putting the Commandments there to be seen, just as the monument's inscription is there for those who walk by it.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To be sure, Kentucky's compulsory-education law meant that the schoolchildren were forced to see the display every day, whereas many see the monument by choice, and those who customarily walk the Capitol grounds can presumably avoid it if they choose.  But in my judgment (and under our often inexact Establishment Clause jurisprudence, such matters often boil down to judgment, see <em>ante</em>, at 3-4 (<em>Breyer, J.,</em> concurring in judgment)), this distinction should make no difference.  The monument in this case sits on the grounds of the Texas State Capitol.  There is something significant in the common term "statehouse" to refer to a state capitol building: it is the civic home of every one of the State's citizens.  If neutrality in religion means something, any citizen should be able to visit that civic home without having to confront religious expressions clearly meant to convey an official religious position that may be at odds with his own religion, or with rejection of religion.  See <em>County of Allegheny</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S., at 626 (<em>O'Connor, J.</em>, concurring in part and concurring in judgment) ("I agree that the cr&amp;ccaron;che displayed on the Grand Staircase of the Allegheny County Courthouse, the seat of county government, conveys a message to nonadherents of Christianity that they are not full members of the political community ...&nbsp;.  The display of religious symbols in public areas of core government buildings runs a special risk of making religion relevant, in reality or public perception, to status in the political community" (alteration and internal quotation marks omitted)).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, though this too is a point on which judgment will vary, I do not see a persuasive argument for constitutionality in the plurality's observation that Van Orden's lawsuit comes "[f]orty years after the monument's erection . . . ," a<em>nte, </em>at 2, an observation that echoes the State's contention that one fact cutting in its favor is that "the monument stood ... in Austin . . . for some forty years without generating any controversy or litigation," Brief for Respondents 25.  It is not that I think the passage of time is necessarily irrelevant in Establishment Clause analysis.  We have approved framing-era practices because they must originally have been understood as constitutionally permissible, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Marsh</em> v. <em>Chambers</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 783 (1983) (legislative prayer), and we have recognized that Sunday laws have grown recognizably secular<em> </em>over time, <em>McGowan</em> v. <em>Maryland,</em> 366 U.&nbsp;S. 420 (1961).  There is also an analogous argument, not yet evaluated, that ritualistic religious expression can become so numbing over time that its initial Establishment Clause violation becomes at some point too diminished for notice.  But I do not understand any of these to be the State's argument, which rather seems to be that 40 years without a challenge shows that as a factual matter the religious expression is too tepid to provoke a serious reaction and constitute a violation.  Perhaps, but the writer of Exodus chapter 20 was not lukewarm, and other explanations may do better in accounting for the late resort to the courts.  Suing a State over religion puts nothing in a plaintiff's pocket and can take a great deal out, and even with volunteer litigators to supply time and energy, the risk of social ostracism can be powerfully deterrent.  I doubt that a slow walk to the courthouse, even one that took 40 years, is much evidentiary help in applying the Establishment Clause.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I would reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeals.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The monuments are: Heroes of the Alamo, Hood's Brigade, Confederate Soldiers, Volunteer Fireman, Terry's Texas Rangers, Texas Cowboy, Spanish-American War, Texas National Guard, Ten Commandments, Tribute to Texas School Children, Texas Pioneer Woman, The Boy Scouts' Statue of Liberty Replica, Pearl Harbor Veterans, Korean War Veterans, Soldiers of World War I, Disabled Veterans, and Texas Peace Officers.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Engel</em> v. <em>Vitale,</em> 370 U.&nbsp;S. 421, 434 (1962) ("The history of man is inseparable from the history of religion"); <em>Zorach</em> v. <em>Clauson,</em> 343 U.&nbsp;S. 306, 313 (1952) ("We are a religious people whose institutions presuppose a Supreme Being").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Despite <em>Justice Stevens'</em> recitation of occasional language to the contrary, <em>post</em>, at 4-5, and n. 7 (dissenting opinion), we have not, and do not, adhere to the principle that the Establishment Clause bars any and all governmental preference for religion over irreligion.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Cutter</em> v.<em> Wilkinson</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S. __ (2005); <em>Corporation of Presiding Bishop of Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</em> v. <em>Amos,</em> 483 U.&nbsp;S. 327 (1987); <em>Lynch</em> v. <em>Donnelly</em>, 465 U.&nbsp;S. 668 (1984); <em>Marsh</em> v. <em>Chambers, </em>463 U.&nbsp;S. 783 (1983);<em> Walz</em> v. <em>Tax Comm'n of City of New York,</em> 397 U.&nbsp;S. 664 (1970).  Even the dissenters do not claim that the First Amendment's Religion Clauses forbid all governmental acknowledgments, preferences, or accommodations of religion.  See <em>post</em>, at 6 (opinion of <em>Stevens, J.</em>) (recognizing that the Establishment Clause permits some "recognition" or "acknowledgment" of religion); <em>post</em>, at 5, and n.&nbsp;4 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.) (discussing a number of permissible displays with religious content).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Zelman</em> v. <em>Simmons-Harris</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 639 (2002) (upholding school voucher program);<em> Good News Club</em> v. <em>Milford Central School,</em> 533 U.&nbsp;S. 98 (2001) (holding that allowing religious school groups to use school facilities does not violate the Establishment Clause);  <em>Agostini</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203 (1997) (approving a program that provided public employees to teach remedial classes at religious and other private schools), overruling <em>Aguilar</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 473 U.&nbsp;S. 402 (1985) (barring public school teachers from going to parochial schools to provide remedial education to disadvantaged children), and <em>School Dist. of Grand Rapids</em> v. <em>Ball,</em> 473 U.&nbsp;S. 373 (1985) (striking down a program that provided classes to religious school students at public expense in classrooms leased from religious schools); <em>Rosenberger</em> v. <em>Rector and Visitors of Univ. of Va.</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 819 (1995) (holding that the Establishment Clause does not bar disbursement of funds from student activity fees to religious organizations); <em>Zobrest</em> v. <em>Catalina Foothills School Dist.</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1993) (allowing a public school district to provide a sign-language interpreter to a deaf student at a Catholic high school as part of a federal program for the disabled); <em>Lynch</em> v. <em>Donnelly, supra</em> (upholding a Christmas display including a cr&amp;ccaron;che); <em>Marsh</em> v. <em>Chambers, supra</em> (upholding legislative prayer); <em>Mueller</em> v. <em>Allen</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 388 (1983) (upholding tax deduction for certain expenses incurred in sending one's child to a religious school).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Santa Fe Independent School Dist.</em> v. <em>Doe</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 290 (2000) (holding unconstitutional student-initiated and student-led prayer at school football games); <em>Board of Ed. of Kiryas Joel</em> <em>Village School Dist.</em> v. <em>Grumet</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S. 687 (1994) (invalidating a state law that created a new school district for a single religious community); <em>Lee</em> v. <em>Weisman</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 577 (1992) (prohibiting officially sponsored graduation prayers); <em>County of Allegheny </em>v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union, Greater Pittsburgh Chapter,</em> 492 U.&nbsp;S. 573 (1989) (holding the display of a cr&amp;ccaron;che in a courthouse unconstitutional but allowing the display of a menorah outside a county building); <em>Texas Monthly, Inc. </em>v. <em>Bullock</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1989) (plurality opinion) (invalidating a sales tax exemption for all religious periodicals); <em>Edwards</em> v. <em>Aguillard</em>, 482 U.&nbsp;S. 578 (1987) (invalidating a law mandating the teaching of creationism if evolution was taught); <em>Estate of Thornton</em> v. <em>Caldor, Inc.</em>, 472 U.&nbsp;S. 703 (1985) (invalidating state law that gave employees an absolute right not to work on their Sabbath); <em>Wallace</em> v. <em>Jaffree</em>, 472 U.&nbsp;S. 38 (1985) (invalidating law mandating a daily minute of silence for meditation or voluntary prayer).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Lemon</em> sets out a three-prong test: "First, the statute must have a secular legislative purpose; second, its principal or primary effect must be one that neither advances nor inhibits religion; finally, the statute must not foster 'an excessive government entanglement with religion.'&nbsp;" 403 U.&nbsp;S., at 612-613 (citation omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Elk Grove Unified School Dist.</em> v. <em>Newdow</em>, 542 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 26 (2004) (<em>Rehnquist</em>, C.&nbsp;J., concurring in judgment) ("Examples of patriotic invocations of God and official acknowledgments of religion's role in our Nation's history abound"); <em>id.</em>, at 35-36 (<em>O'Connor</em>, J., concurring in judgment) ("It is unsurprising that a Nation founded by religious refugees and dedicated to religious freedom should find references to divinity in its symbols, songs, mottoes, and oaths"); <em>Lynch</em> v. <em>Donnelly,</em> 465 U.&nbsp;S., at 675 ("Our history is replete with official references to the value and invocation of Divine guidance").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, we rejected the claim that an Establishment Clause violation was presented because the prayers had once been offered in the Judeo-Christian tradition: In <em>Marsh</em>, the prayers were often explicitly Christian, but the chaplain removed all references to Christ the year after the suit was filed.  463 U.&nbsp;S.,<em> </em>at 793-794, and n. 14.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Other examples of monuments and buildings reflecting the prominent role of religion abound.  For example, the Washington, Jefferson, and Lincoln Memorials all contain explicit invocations of God's importance.  The apex of the Washington Monument is inscribed "Laus Deo," which is translated to mean "Praise be to God," and multiple memorial stones in the monument contain Biblical citations.  The Jefferson Memorial is engraved with three quotes from Jefferson that make God a central theme.  Inscribed on the wall of the Lincoln Memorial are two of Lincoln's most famous speeches, the Gettysburg Address and his Second Inaugural Address.  Both inscriptions include those speeches' extensive acknowledgments of God.  The first federal monument, which was accepted by the United States in honor of sailors who died in Tripoli, noted the dates of the fallen sailors as "the year of our Lord, 1804, and in the 28 year of the independence of the United States."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Edwards</em> v. <em>Aguillard,</em> 449 U.&nbsp;S. 39, 45 (1980) (<em>Rehnquist</em>, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nor does anything suggest that <em>Stone</em> would extend to displays of the Ten Commandments that lack a "plainly religious," "pre-eminent purpose," <em>id.,</em> at 41.  See <em>Edwards</em> v. <em>Aguillard,</em> <em>supra</em>, at 593-594 ("<em>[Stone]</em> did not mean that no use could ever be made of the Ten Commandments, or that the Ten Commandments played an exclusively religious role in the history of Western Civilization").  Indeed, we need not decide in this case the extent to which a primarily religious purpose would affect our analysis because it is clear from the record that there is no evidence of such a purpose in this case.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" href="#FRconcurrence2.*">*</a></strong><br><p>*&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>County of Allegheny </em>v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union, Greater Pittsburgh Chapter,</em> 492 U.&nbsp;S. 573, 592-594 (1989) (employing endorsement test); <em>Lemon</em> v. <em>Kurtzman</em>, 403 U.&nbsp;S. 602, 612-613 (1971) (setting forth three-pronged test); <em>Marsh</em> v. <em>Chambers,</em> 463 U.&nbsp;S. 783, 790-792 (1983) (upholding legislative prayer due to its "unique history"); see also <em>Lynch</em> v. <em>Donnelly</em>, 465 U.&nbsp;S. 668, 679-681 (1984) ("[W]e have repeatedly emphasized our unwillingness to be confined to any single test or criterion in this sensitive area").</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;At the bottom of the message, the observer learns that the display was "[p]resented to the people and youth of Texas by the Fraternal Order of Eagles of Texas" in 1961.  See Appendix, <em>infra</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Reynolds</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 98 U.&nbsp;S. 145, 164 (1879); see also <em>Everson</em> v. <em>Board of Ed. of Ewing</em>, 330 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 16 (1947).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As Senator Danforth recently reminded us, "efforts to haul references of God into the public square, into schools and courthouses, are far more apt to divide Americans than to advance faith."  Danforth, Onward, Moderate Christian Soldiers, N.&nbsp;Y. Times, June 17, 2005, p.&nbsp;A27.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent1.4" href="#FRdissent1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The accuracy and utility of this metaphor have been called into question.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Wallace</em> v. <em>Jaffree</em>, 472 U.&nbsp;S. 38, 106 (1985) (<em>Rehnquist</em>, J., dissenting); see generally P. Hamburger, Separation of Church and State (2002).  Whatever one may think of the merits of the historical debate surrounding Jefferson and the "wall" metaphor, this Court at a minimum has never questioned the concept of the "separation of church and state" in our First Amendment jurisprudence.  <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em>'s opinion affirms that principle.  <em>Ante</em>, at 4 (demanding a "separation between church and state").  Indeed, even the Court that famously opined that "[w]e are a religious people whose institutions presuppose a Supreme Being," <em>Zorach</em> v. <em>Clauson</em>, 343 U.&nbsp;S. 306, 313 (1952), acknowledged that "[t]here cannot be the slightest doubt that the First Amendment reflects the philosophy that Church and State should be separated," <em>id.</em>, at 312.  The question we face is how to give meaning to that concept of separation.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is now widespread consensus on this principle.  See <em>Everson</em> v. <em>Board of Ed. of Ewing</em>, 330 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 15 (1947) ("Neither a state nor the Federal Government . . . can pass laws which aid one religion, aid all religions, or prefer one religion over another"); <em>School District of Abington Township </em>v.<em> Schempp</em>, 374 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 226 (1963) ("In the relationship between man and religion, the State is firmly committed to a position of neutrality"); <em>Larson</em> v. <em>Valente</em>, 456 U.&nbsp;S. 228, 244 (1982) ("The clearest command of the Establishment Clause is that one religious denomination cannot be officially preferred over another"); see also <em>Board of Ed. of Kiryas Joel Village School Dist.</em> v. <em>Grumet,</em> 512 U.&nbsp;S. 687, 748 (1994) (<em>Scalia</em>, J., dissenting) ("I have always believed ... that the Establishment Clause prohibits the favoring of one religion over others"); but see <em>Church of Holy Trinity</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 143 U.&nbsp;S. 457, 470-471 (1892).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In support of this proposition, the <em>Torcaso </em>Court quoted James Iredell, who in the course of debating the adoption of the Federal Constitution in North Carolina, stated: "&nbsp;'it is objected that the people of America may perhaps choose representatives who have no religion at all, and that Pagans and Mahometans may be admitted into offices.  But how is it possible to exclude any set of men, without taking away that principle of religious freedom which we ourselves so warmly contend for?'&nbsp;"  367 U.&nbsp;S., at 495, n.&nbsp;10 (quoting 4 J. Elliot, Debates in the Several State Conventions on the Adoption of the Federal Constitution 197 (1836 ed.)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent1.7" href="#FRdissent1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Everson</em>, 330 U.&nbsp;S., at 18 (the Establishment Clause "requires the state to be ... neutral in its relations with groups of religious believers and non-believers"); <em>Abington</em>, 374 U.&nbsp;S., at 216 (rejecting the proposition that the Establishment Clause "forbids only governmental preference of one religion over another"); <em>Wallace</em>, 472 U.&nbsp;S., at 52-55 (the interest in "forestalling intolerance extends beyond intolerance among Christian sects--or even intolerance among 'religions'--to encompass intolerance of the disbeliever and the uncertain"); cf. <em>Zorach</em>, 343 U.&nbsp;S., at 325 (Jackson, J., dissenting) ("The day that this country ceases to be free for irreligion it will cease to be free for religion--except for the sect that can win political power").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNdissent1.8" href="#FRdissent1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Scalia</em>'s dissent in the other Ten Commandments case we decide today, see <em>McCreary County </em>v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union of Ky</em>., <em>post</em>, at 1-11, raises similar objections.  I address these objections directly in Part III.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNdissent1.9" href="#FRdissent1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Though this Court has subscribed to the view that the Ten Commandments influenced the development of Western legal thought, it has not officially endorsed the far more specific claim that the Ten Commandments played a significant role in the development of our Nation's foundational documents (and the subsidiary implication that it has special relevance to Texas).  Although it is perhaps an overstatement to characterize this latter proposition as "idiotic," see Tr. of Oral Arg. 34, as one Member of the <em>plurality </em>has done, at the very least the question is a matter of intense scholarly debate.  Compare Brief for Legal Historians and Law Scholars as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> in <em>McCreary County</em> v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union of Ky.</em>, O. T. 2004, No. 03-1693, with<em> </em>Brief for American Center for Law and Justice as <em>Amici Curiae</em>.  Whatever the historical accuracy of the proposition, the District Court categorically rejected respondent's suggestion that the State's actual<em> </em>purpose in displaying the Decalogue was to signify its influence on secular law and Texas institutions.  App. to Pet. for Cert. A-32.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNdissent1.10" href="#FRdissent1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for Fraternal Order of Eagles as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 2-3.  The Order was formed in 1898 by six Seattle theater owners, promptly joined by actors, playwrights, and stagehands, and rapidly expanded to include a nationwide membership numbering over a million.  <em>Id.</em>, at 2; see also <em>Fraternal Order of Eagles </em>v. <em>Grand Aerie of Fraternal Order of Eagles</em>, 148 Wash. 2d 224, 229, 59 P.&nbsp;3d 655, 657 (2002) (en banc); <em>Lahmann </em>v. <em>Grand Aerie of Fraternal Order of Eagles</em>, 180 Ore. App. 420, 422, 43 P.&nbsp;3d 1130, 1131 (2002).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNdissent1.11" href="#FRdissent1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Books </em>v.<em> Elkhart</em>, 235 F.&nbsp;3d 292, 294-295 (CA7 2000); <em>State </em>v. <em>Freedom from Religion Foundation, Inc.</em>, 898 P.&nbsp;2d 1013, 1017 (Colo. 1995) (en banc); see also U.&nbsp;S. Supreme Court will hear Ten Commandments Case in Early 2005, http://www.foe.com/tencommandments/<br>index.html (all Internet materials as visited June 24, 2005, and available in Clerk of Court's case file).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNdissent1.12" href="#FRdissent1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Freedom from Religion Foundation</em>, 898 P.&nbsp;2d, at 1017; accord, 1961 Tex. Gen. Laws 1995 ("These plaques and monoliths have been presented by the Eagles to promote youth morality and to help stop the alarming increase in delinquency"); Brief for Fraternal Order of Eagles as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 4.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNdissent1.13" href="#FRdissent1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;According to its articles of incorporation, the Eagles' purpose is to: "&nbsp;'Unite fraternally for mutual benefit, protection, improvement, social enjoyment and association, all persons of good moral character who believe in a Supreme Being to inculcate the principles of liberty, truth, justice and equality ...'&nbsp;"  <em>Fraternal Order of Eagles</em>, 148 Wash. 2d, at 229, 59 P.&nbsp;3d, at 657.  See also Aerie Membership Application-Fraternal Order of Eagles http://www.foe.com/membership/applications/aerie.html ("I, being of sound body and mind, and believing in the existence of a Supreme Being ...").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNdissent1.14" href="#FRdissent1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>County of Allegheny</em> v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union, Greater Pittsburgh Chapter</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S. 573 (1989)<em>, </em>I noted that certain displays of religious images may convey "an equivocal message, perhaps of respect for Judaism, for religion in general, or for law."  <em>Id.</em>, at 652 (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part).  It is rather misleading, however, to quote my comment in that case to imply that I was referring to the text of the Ten Commandments <em>simpliciter</em>.  See <em>McCreary County</em>,<em> post</em>, at 13-14.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNdissent1.15" href="#FRdissent1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>ante</em>, at 5 (<em>Breyer</em>, J., concurring in judgment).  Despite the Eagles' efforts, not all of the monuments they donated in fact conform to a "universally-accepted" text.  Compare, <em>e.g.</em>, Appendix, <em>infra</em> (including the command that "Thou shalt not make to thyself any graven images"), and<em> Adland </em>v. <em>Russ</em>, 307 F.&nbsp;3d 471, 475 (CA6 2002) (same), with <em>Freedom from Religion Foundation</em>, 898 P.&nbsp;2d, at 1016 (omitting that command altogether).  The distinction represents a critical divide between the Protestant and Catholic faiths.  During the Reformation, Protestants destroyed images of the Virgin Mary and of Jesus Christ that were venerated in Catholic churches. Even today there is a notable difference between the imagery in different churches, a difference that may in part be attributable to differing understandings of the meaning of what is the Second Commandment in the King James Bible translation and a portion of the First Commandment in the Catholic translation.  See Finkelman, The Ten Commandments on the Courthouse Lawn and Elsewhere, 73 Ford. L.&nbsp;Rev. 1477, 1493-1494 (2005).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNdissent1.16" href="#FRdissent1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For example, in the Jewish version of the Sixth Commandment God commands: "You shall not murder"; whereas, the King James interpretation of the same command is: "Thou shalt not kill."  Compare W. Plaut, The Torah: A Modern Commentary 534 (1981), with<em> </em>Appendix, <em>infra</em>.  The difference between the two versions is not merely semantic; rather, it is but one example of a deep theological dispute.  See Finkelman, <em>supra</em>, at 1481-1500; P. Maier, Enumerating the Decalogue; Do We Number the Ten Commandments Correctly?  16 Concordia J. 18, 18-26 (1990).  Varying interpretations of this Commandment explain the actions of vegetarians who refuse to eat meat, pacifists who refuse to work for munitions makers, prison officials who refuse to administer lethal injections to death row inmates, and pharmacists who refuse to sell morning-after pills to women.  See Finkelman, <em>supra</em>, at 1494-1496; Brief for American Jewish Congress et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 22-23.  Although the command is ambiguous, its power to motivate likeminded interpreters of its message cannot be denied.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNdissent1.17" href="#FRdissent1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Scalia'</em>s willingness to dismiss the distinct textual versions adhered to by different faiths in the name of generic "monotheism" based on mere speculation regarding their significance, <em>McCreary County, post</em>, at 19, is not only somewhat ironic, see A. Scalia, A Matter of Interpretation 23-25 (1997), but also serves to reinforce the concern that interjecting government into the religious sphere will offend "adherents who consider the particular advertisement disrespectful."  <em>Allegheny County</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S., at 651 (<em>Stevens, J., </em>concurring in part and dissenting in part).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNdissent1.18" href="#FRdissent1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for Hindu American Foundation et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em>.  Though <em>Justice Scalia</em> disagrees that these sentiments are consistent with the Establishment Clause, he does not deny that our cases wholeheartedly adopt this expression of neutrality.  Instead, he suggests that this Court simply discard what he terms the "say-so of earlier Courts," based in part on his own "say-so" that nonmonotheists make up a statistically insignificant portion of this Nation's religious community.  <em>McCreary County, post</em>, at 6.  Besides marginalizing the belief systems of more than 7 million Americans by deeming them unworthy of the special protections he offers monotheists under the Establishment Clause, <em>Justice Scalia</em>'s measure of analysis may be cause for concern even for the self-proclaimed "popular" religions of Islam and Judaism.  The number of Buddhists alone is nearly equal to the number of Muslims in this country, and while those of the Islamic and Jewish faiths only account for 2.2% of all believers, Christianity accounts for 95.5%.  See U.&nbsp;S. Dept. of Commerce, Bureau of Census, Statistical Abstract of the United States: 2004-2005, p.&nbsp;55 (124th ed. 2004) (Table No. 67).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNdissent1.19" href="#FRdissent1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The fact that this particular display has stood unchallenged for over forty years does not suggest otherwise.  One need look no further than the deluge of cases flooding lower courts to realize the discord these displays have engendered.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Mercier </em>v. <em>Fraternal Order of Eagles</em>, 395 F.&nbsp;3d 693 (CA7 2005); <em>ACLU Nebraska Foundation </em>v. <em>Plattsmouth</em>, 358 F.&nbsp;3d 1020 (CA8 2004); <em>Adland </em>v. <em>Russ</em>, 307 F.&nbsp;3d 471 (CA6 2002); <em>Summum </em>v. <em>Ogden</em>, 297 F.&nbsp;3d 995 (CA10 2002); <em>Books </em>v. <em>Elkhart</em>, 235 F.&nbsp;3d 292 (CA7 2000); <em>State </em>v. <em>Freedom From Religion Foundation, Inc.,</em> 898 P.&nbsp;2d 1013 (Colo. 1995); <em>Anderson </em>v. <em>Salt Lake City Corp.</em>, 475 F.&nbsp;2d 29 (CA10 1973).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNdissent1.20" href="#FRdissent1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This is, of course, a rhetorical approach not unique to the plurality's opinion today.  Appeals to such religious speeches have frequently been used in support of governmental transmission of religious messages.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Wallace</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 577, 633-636 (1992) (<em>Scalia</em>, J., dissenting); <em>Santa&nbsp;Fe Independent School Dist. </em>v. <em>Doe</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 290, 318 (2000) (<em>Rehnquist</em>, C.&nbsp;J., dissenting); cf. <em>Lynch</em> v. <em>Donnelly</em>, 465 U.&nbsp;S. 668, 675-676 (1984).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNdissent1.21" href="#FRdissent1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It goes without saying that the analysis differs when a listener is  coerced into listening to a prayer.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Santa&nbsp;Fe Independent School Dist., </em>530 U.&nbsp;S., at  308-312.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNdissent1.22" href="#FRdissent1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;With respect to the "legislative prayers" cited approvingly by <em>The Chief Justice</em>, <em>ante</em>, at 8, I reiterate my view that "the designation of a member of one religious faith to serve as the sole official chaplain of a state legislature for a period of 16 years constitutes the preference of one faith over another in violation of the Establishment Clause."  <em>Marsh</em> v. <em>Chambers</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 783, 823 (1983) (<em>Stevens</em>, J., dissenting).  Thus, <em>Justice Scalia</em> and I are in agreement with respect to at least one point--this Court's decision in <em>Marsh </em>"ignor[ed] the neutrality principle" at the heart of the Establishment Clause.  <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 8 <em>(Scalia</em>, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNdissent1.23" href="#FRdissent1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> J. Hutson, Religion and the Founding of the American Republic 75 (1998) (noting the dearth of references to God at the Philadelphia Convention and that many contemporaneous observers of the Convention complained that "the Framers had unaccountably turned their backs on the Almighty" because they "&nbsp;'found the Constitution without any acknowledgement of God'&nbsp;").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNdissent1.24" href="#FRdissent1.24">24</a></strong><br><p> See Letter from Thomas Jefferson to Rev. S. Miller (Jan. 23, 1808), in 5 Founders' Constitution 98; 11 Jefferson's Writings 428-430 (1905); see also <em>Lee</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 623-625 (<em>Souter</em>, J., concurring) (documenting history); <em>Lynch</em>, 465 U.&nbsp;S., at 716, n. 23 (Brennan, J., dissenting) (same).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNdissent1.25" href="#FRdissent1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also James Madison, Detached Memoranda, in 5 Founders' Constitution 103-104.  Madison's letter to Livingston further argued that: "There has been another deviation from the strict principle in the Executive Proclamations of fasts &amp; festivals, so far, at least, as they have spoken the language of <em>injunction</em>, or have lost sight of the equality of <em>all </em>religious sects in the eve of the Constitution...&nbsp;. Notwithstanding the general progress made within the last two centuries in favour of this branch of liberty, &amp; the full establishment of it, in some parts of our Country, there remains in others a strong bias towards old error, that without some sort of alliance or coalition between [Government] &amp; Religion neither can be duly supported.  Such indeed is the tendency to such a coalition, and such its corrupting influence on both the parties, that the danger cannot be too carefully guarded [against]...&nbsp;. Every new &amp; successful example therefore of a perfect separation between ecclesiastical and civil matters, is of importance.  And I have no doubt that every new example, will succeed, as every past one has done, in shewing that religion &amp; [Government] will both exist in greater purity, the less they are mixed together."  <em>Id.</em>, at 105-106.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNdissent1.26" href="#FRdissent1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Religion and Politics in the Early Republic 20-21 (D. Dreisbach ed. 1996) (hereinafter Dreisbach) (quoting Letter from James Madison to Jasper Adams (1833)).  See also Letter from James Madison to Edward Livingston (July 10, 1822), in 5 Founders' Constitution 106 ("We are teaching the world the great truth that [Governments] do better without Kings &amp; Nobles than with them.  The merit will be doubled by the other lesson that Religion flourishes in greater purity, without than with the aid of [Government]").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNdissent1.27" href="#FRdissent1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The contrary evidence cited by <em>The</em> <em>Chief Justice</em> and <em>Justice Scalia </em>only underscores the obvious fact that leaders who have drafted and voted for a text are eminently capable of violating their own rules.  The first Congress was--just as the present Congress is--capable of passing unconstitutional legislation.  Thus, it is no answer to say that the Founders' separationist impulses were "plainly rejected" simply because the first Congress enacted laws that acknowledged God.  See <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 13 (<em>Scalia</em>, J., dissenting).  To adopt such an interpretive approach would misguidedly give authoritative weight to the fact that the Congress that passed the Fourteenth Amendment also enacted laws that tolerated segregation, and the fact that the Congress that passed the First Amendment also enacted laws, such as the Alien and Sedition Act, that indisputably violated our present understanding of the First Amendment.  See n. 36, <em>infra</em>; <em>Lee</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 626 (<em>Souter</em>, J., concurring).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNdissent1.28" href="#FRdissent1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> Strang, The Meaning of "Religion" in the First Amendment, 40 Duquesne L.&nbsp;Rev. 181, 220-223 (2002).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNdissent1.29" href="#FRdissent1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Justice Story wrote elsewhere that "&nbsp;'Christianity is indispensable to the true interests &amp; solid foundations of all free governments.  I distinguish ... between the establishment of a particular sect, as the Religion of the State, &amp; the Establishment of Christianity itself, without any preference of any particular form of it.  I know not, indeed, how any deep sense of moral obligation or accountableness can be expected to prevail in the community without a firm persuasion of the great Christian Truths."  Letter to Jasper Adams (May 14, 1833) Dreisbach 19.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNdissent1.30" href="#FRdissent1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 143 U.&nbsp;S., at 471<em> </em>("&nbsp;'[W]e are a Christian people, and the morality of the country is deeply ingrafted upon Christianity, and not upon the doctrines or worship of ... imposters'&nbsp;" (quoting <em>People </em>v. <em>Ruggles</em>, 8 Johns. 290, 295 (N.&nbsp;Y. Sup. Ct. 1811))); see also <em>Vidal</em> v. <em>Philadelphia,</em> 2 How. 127, 198-199 (1844).  These views should not be read as those of religious zealots.  Chief Justice Marshall himself penned the historical genesis of the Court's assertion that our "&nbsp;'institutions presuppose a Supreme Being,'&nbsp;" see <em>Zorach</em>, 343 U.&nbsp;S., at 313, writing that the "American population is entirely Christian, &amp; with us, Christianity &amp; Religion are identified.  It would be strange, indeed, if with such a people, our institutions did not presuppose Christianity, &amp; did not often refer to it, &amp; exhibit relations with it."  Letter from John Marshall to Jasper Adams (May 9, 1833) (quoted in Dreisbach 18-19).  Accord, Story §988, p. 700 ("[A]t the time of the adoption of the constitution, . . . the general, if not the universal, sentiment in America was, that Christianity ought to receive encouragement from the state ..." (footnote omitted)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNdissent1.31" href="#FRdissent1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Scalia</em>'s characterization of this conclusion as nothing more than my own personal "assurance" is misleading to say the least.  <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 13.  Reliance on our Nation's early constitutional scholars is common in this Court's opinions.  In particular, the author of the plurality once noted that "Joseph Story, a Member of this Court from 1811 to 1845, and during much of that time a professor at the Harvard Law School, published by far the most comprehensive treatise on the United States Constitution that had then appeared."  <em>Wallace</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 506, 510-511 (1995) (Fifth Amendment); <em>Harmelin</em> v. <em>Michigan</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 957, 981-982 (1991) (Eighth Amendment).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNdissent1.32" href="#FRdissent1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Scalia</em>'s answer--that incorporation does not empty "the incorporated provisions of their original meaning," <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 15--ignores the fact that the Establishment Clause has its own unique history.  There is no evidence, for example, that incorporation of the Confrontation Clause ran contrary to the core of the Clause's original understanding.  There is, however, some persuasive evidence to this effect regarding the Establishment Clause.  See <em>Elk Grove Unified School Dist.</em> v. <em>Newdow, </em>542 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 49 (2004) (<em>Thomas</em>, J., concurring in judgment) (arguing that the Clause was originally understood to be a "federalism provision" intended to prevent "Congress from interfering with state establishments").  It is this unique history, not incorporation writ large, that renders incoherent the postincorporation reliance on the Establishment Clause's original understanding.</p>
<p>

<em>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Justice Thomas</em>, at least, has faced this problem head-on.  See <em>id.</em>, at 45 (opinion concurring in judgment).  But even if the decision to incorporate the Establishment Clause was misguided, it is at this point unwise to reverse course given the weight of precedent that would have to be cast aside to reach the intended result.  See Cardozo, The Nature of the Judicial Process 149 (1937) ("The labor of judges would be increased almost to the breaking point if every past decision could be reopened in every case").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNdissent1.33" href="#FRdissent1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Lee</em>, 465 U.&nbsp;S. 668, 716 (1984) (Brennan, J., dissenting) (same); cf. Feldman, Intellectual Origins of the Establishment Clause, 77 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;U.&nbsp;L.&nbsp;Rev. 346, 404-405 (2002) (noting that, for the Framers, "the term 'establishment' was a contested one" and that the word "was used in both narrow and expansive ways in the debates of the time").</p>
