<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNdissent1.34" href="#FRdissent1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Hovenkamp, The Cultural Crises of the Fuller Court, 104 Yale L.&nbsp;J. 2309, 2337-2342 (1995) ("Equal protection had not been identified with social integration when the Fourteenth Amendment was drafted in 1866, nor when it was ratified in 1868, nor when <em>Plessy</em> [v. <em>Ferguson</em>, 163 U.&nbsp;S. 537] was decided in 1896"); see also 1 L. Tribe, American Constitutional Law §1-14, pp. 54-55, and n. 19 (3d ed. 2000) (collecting scholarship).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNdissent1.35" href="#FRdissent1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Justice Thomas</em> contends that the Establishment Clause cannot include such a neutrality principle because the Clause reaches only the governmental coercion of individual belief or disbelief.  <em>Ante</em>, at 4 (concurring opinion).  In my view, although actual religious coercion is undoubtedly forbidden by the Establishment Clause, that cannot be the full extent of the provision's reach.  Jefferson's "wall" metaphor and his refusal to issue Thanksgiving proclamations, see <em>supra, </em>at 19, would have been nonsensical if the Clause reached only direct coercion.  Further, under the "coercion" view, the Establishment Clause would amount to little more than a replica of our compelled speech doctrine, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>West Virginia Bd. of Ed.</em> v. <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U. S. 624, 639 (1943), with a religious flavor.  A Clause so interpreted would not prohibit explicit state endorsements of religious orthodoxies of particular sects, actions that lie at the heart of what the Clause was meant to regulate.  The government could, for example, take out television advertisements lauding Catholicism as the only pure religion.  Under the reasoning endorsed by <em>Justice Thomas</em>, those programs would not be coercive because the viewer could simply turn off the television or ignore the ad.  See <em>ante</em>, at 3 ("[T]he mere presence of the monument ... involves no coercion" because the passerby "need not stop to read it or even to look at it").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Further, the notion that the application of a "coercion" principle would somehow lead to a more consistent jurisprudence is dubious.  Enshrining coercion as the Establishment Clause touchstone fails to eliminate the difficult judgment calls regarding "the form that coercion must take."  <em>McCreary County</em>, <em>post</em>, at 25 (<em>Scalia</em>, J., dissenting).  Coercion may seem obvious to some, while appearing nonexistent to others.  Compare <em>Santa&nbsp;Fe Independent School Dist.</em>, 370 U.&nbsp;S. 421, 431 (1962) ("When the power, prestige and financial support of government is placed behind a particular religious belief, the indirect coercive pressure upon religious minorities to conform to the prevailing officially approved religion is plain").  In short, "reasonable people could, and no doubt would, argue about whether coercion existed in a particular situation."  Feldman, The Intellectual Origins of the Establishment Clause, 77 N.&nbsp;Y. U. L.&nbsp;Rev. 346, 415 (2002).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent3.1" href="#FRdissent3.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The clarity of the religious manifestation in <em>Stone</em> was unaffected by the State's effort to obscure it: the Kentucky statute that mandated posting the Commandments in classrooms also required the addition to every posting of a notation reading, "[t]he secular application of the Ten Commandments is clearly seen in its adoption as the fundamental legal code of Western Civilization and the Common Law of the United States."  449 U.&nbsp;S., at 39-40, n.&nbsp;1.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the present case, the religious purpose was evident on the part<br>of the donating organization.  When the Fraternal Order of Eagles, the group that gave the monument to the State of Texas, donated identical monuments to other jurisdictions, it was seeking to impart a religious message.  See <em>Adland</em> v. <em>Russ</em>, 307 F.&nbsp;3d 471, 475 (CA6 2002) (quoting the Eagles' statement in a letter written to Kentucky when a monument was donated to that Commonwealth: "Most of today's younger generation either have not seen the Ten Commandments or have not been taught them.  In our opinion the youth of today is in dire need of learning the simple laws of God . . .").  Accordingly, it was not just the terms of the moral code, but the proclamation that the terms of the code were enjoined by God, that the Eagles put forward in the monuments they donated.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent3.2" href="#FRdissent3.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;That the monument also surrounds the text of the Commandments with various American symbols (notably the U.&nbsp;S. flag and a bald eagle) only underscores the impermissibility of Texas's actions: by juxtaposing these patriotic symbols with the Commandments and other religious signs, the monument sends the message that being American means being religious (and not just being religious but also subscribing to the Commandments, <em>i.e.,</em> practicing a monotheistic religion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent3.3" href="#FRdissent3.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is no question that the State in its own right is broadcasting the religious message.  When Texas accepted the monument from the Eagles, the state legislature, aware that the Eagles "for the past several years have placed across the country . . . parchment plaques and granite monoliths of the Ten Commandments . . . [in order] to promote youth morality and help stop the alarming increase in delinquency," resolved "that the Fraternal Order of the Eagles of the State of Texas be commended and congratulated for its efforts and contributions in combating juvenile delinquency throughout our nation."  App. 97.  The State, then, expressly approved of the Eagles' proselytizing, which it made on its own.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent3.4" href="#FRdissent3.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For similar reasons, the other displays of the Commandments that the plurality mentions, <em>ante</em>, at 9, do not run afoul of the Establishment Clause.  The statues of Moses and St. Paul in the Main Reading Room of the Library of Congress are 2 of 16 set in close proximity, statues that "represent men illustrious in the various forms of thought and activity ...&nbsp;."  The Library of Congress: The Art and Architecture of the Thomas Jefferson Building 127 (J. Cole and H. Reeds eds. 1997).  Moses and St. Paul represent religion, while the other 14 (a group that includes Beethoven, Shakespeare, Michelangelo, Columbus, and Plato) represent the nonreligious categories of philosophy, art, history, commerce, science, law, and poetry.  <em>Ibid</em>.  Similarly, the sculpture of the woman beside the Decalogue in the Main Reading Room is one of 8 such figures "represent[ing] eight characteristic features of civilized life and thought," the same 8 features (7 of them nonreligious) that Moses, St. Paul, and the rest of the 16 statues represent.  <em>Id</em>., at 125.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The inlay on the floor of the National Archives Building is one of four such discs, the collective theme of which is not religious.  Rather, the discs "symbolize the various types of Government records that were to come into the National Archive."  Letter from Judith A. Koucky, Archivist, Records Control Section to Catherine Millard, Oct. 1, 2003 (on file with Clerk of the Court).   (The four categories are war and defense, history, justice, and legislation.  Each disc is paired with a winged figure; the disc containing the depiction of the Commandments, a depiction that, notably, omits the Commandments' text, is paired with a figure representing legislation.  <em>Ibid</em>.)</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As for Moses's "prominen[t] featur[ing] in the Chamber of the United States House of Representatives," <em>ante</em>, at 9 (plurality opinion), Moses is actually 1 of 23 portraits encircling the House Chamber, each approximately the same size, having no religious theme.  The portraits depict "men noted in history for the part they played in the evolution of what has become American law."  Art in the United States Capitol 282; House Doc. No. 94-660 (1978).  More importantly for purposes of this case, each portrait consists only of the subject's face; the Ten Commandments appear nowhere in Moses's portrait.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent3.5" href="#FRdissent3.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Similarly permissible, though obviously of a different character, are laws that can be traced back to the Commandments (even the more religious ones) but are currently supported by nonreligious considerations.  See <em>McCreary County</em> v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union of Ky.,</em> <em>post</em>, at 10 (opinion of the Court) (noting that in <em>McGowan</em> v. <em>Maryland,</em> 366 U.&nbsp;S. 420 (1961), the Court "upheld Sunday closing laws on practical secular grounds after finding that the government had forsaken the religious purposes motivating centuries-old predecessor laws").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent3.6" href="#FRdissent3.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It is true that the Commandments monument is unlike the display of the Commandments considered in the other Ten Commandments case we decide today, <em>McCreary County</em>.  There the Commandments were posted at the behest of the county in the first instance, whereas the State of Texas received the monument as a gift from the Eagles, which apparently conceived of the donation at the suggestion of a movie producer bent on promoting his commercial film on the Ten Commandments, <em>Books</em> v. <em>Elkhart</em>, 235 F.&nbsp;3d 292, 294-295 (CA7 2000), cert. denied, 532 U.&nbsp;S. 1058 (2001).  But this distinction fails to neutralize the apparent expression of governmental intent to promote a religious message: although the nativity scene in <em>Allegheny County </em>was donated by the Holy Name Society, we concluded that "[n]o viewer could reasonably think that [the scene] occupies [its] location [at the seat of county government] without the support and approval of the government."  <em>County of Allegheny </em>v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union, Greater Pittsburgh Chapter, </em>492 U.&nbsp;S. 573, 599-600 (1989).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent3.7" href="#FRdissent3.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In any event, the fact that we have been, as the plurality says, "particularly vigilant in monitoring compliance with the Establishment Clause in elementary and secondary schools," <em>ante,</em> at 11, does not of course mean that anything goes outside the schoolhouse.  As cases like <em>County of Allegheny </em>and<em> Lynch</em> v. <em>Donnelly,</em> 465 U.&nbsp;S. 668 (1984), illustrate, we have also closely scrutinized government displays of religious symbols.  And for reasons discussed in the text, the Texas monument cannot survive even a relaxed level of scrutiny.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> VAN ORDEN v. PERRY [03-1500], 545 U.S. 677 (2005)</h2>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
});</script><script src="https://www.datadoghq-browser-agent.com/datadog-rum.js" type="text/javascript"></script>
